        <title>Genome sequencing of herb Tulsi ( Ocimum tenuiflorum ) unravels key genes behind its strong medicinal properties | BMC Plant Biology | Full Text</title>

        

    <meta name="citation_abstract" content="Krishna Tulsi, a member of Lamiaceae family, is a herb well known for its spiritual, religious and medicinal importance in India. The common name of this plant is &#8216;Tulsi&#8217; (or &#8216;Tulasi&#8217; or &#8216;Thulasi&#8217;) and is considered sacred by Hindus. We present the draft genome of Ocimum tenuiflurum L (subtype Krishna Tulsi) in this report. The paired-end and mate-pair sequence libraries were generated for the whole genome sequenced with the Illumina Hiseq 1000, resulting in an assembled genome of 374&#160;Mb, with a genome coverage of 61&#160;% (612&#160;Mb estimated genome size). We have also studied transcriptomes (RNA-Seq) of two subtypes of O. tenuiflorum, Krishna and Rama Tulsi and report the relative expression of genes in both the varieties. The pathways leading to the production of medicinally-important specialized metabolites have been studied in detail, in relation to similar pathways in Arabidopsis thaliana and other plants. Expression levels of anthocyanin biosynthesis-related genes in leaf samples of Krishna Tulsi were observed to be relatively high, explaining the purple colouration of Krishna Tulsi leaves. The expression of six important genes identified from genome data were validated by performing q-RT-PCR in different tissues of five different species, which shows the high extent of urosolic acid-producing genes in young leaves of the Rama subtype. In addition, the presence of eugenol and ursolic acid, implied as potential drugs in the cure of many diseases including cancer was confirmed using mass spectrometry. The availability of the whole genome of O.tenuiflorum and our sequence analysis suggests that small amino acid changes at the functional sites of genes involved in metabolite synthesis pathways confer special medicinal properties to this herb."/>

<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

                if (candidate === element) {
                    return true;
                } else if (candidate.nodeName.toLowerCase() === 'body') {
                    return false;

        <meta property="og:title" content="Genome sequencing of herb Tulsi ( Ocimum tenuiflorum ) unravels key genes behind its strong medicinal properties"/>
        <meta property="og:description" content="Krishna Tulsi, a member of Lamiaceae family, is a herb well known for its spiritual, religious and medicinal importance in India. The common name of this plant is ‘Tulsi’ (or ‘Tulasi’ or ‘Thulasi’) and is considered sacred by Hindus. We present the draft genome of Ocimum tenuiflurum L (subtype Krishna Tulsi) in this report. The paired-end and mate-pair sequence libraries were generated for the whole genome sequenced with the Illumina Hiseq 1000, resulting in an assembled genome of 374&amp;nbsp;Mb, with a genome coverage of 61&amp;nbsp;% (612&amp;nbsp;Mb estimated genome size). We have also studied transcriptomes (RNA-Seq) of two subtypes of O. tenuiflorum, Krishna and Rama Tulsi and report the relative expression of genes in both the varieties. The pathways leading to the production of medicinally-important specialized metabolites have been studied in detail, in relation to similar pathways in Arabidopsis thaliana and other plants. Expression levels of anthocyanin biosynthesis-related genes in leaf samples of Krishna Tulsi were observed to be relatively high, explaining the purple colouration of Krishna Tulsi leaves. The expression of six important genes identified from genome data were validated by performing q-RT-PCR in different tissues of five different species, which shows the high extent of urosolic acid-producing genes in young leaves of the Rama subtype. In addition, the presence of eugenol and ursolic acid, implied as potential drugs in the cure of many diseases including cancer was confirmed using mass spectrometry. The availability of the whole genome of O.tenuiflorum and our sequence analysis suggests that small amino acid changes at the functional sites of genes involved in metabolite synthesis pathways confer special medicinal properties to this herb."/>
        <meta property="og:image" content="https://media.springernature.com/w200/springer-static/cover/journal/12870.jpg"/>
    
        
    </head>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Background</h3><p>Krishna Tulsi, a member of Lamiaceae family, is a herb well known for its spiritual, religious and medicinal importance in India. The common name of this plant is ‘Tulsi’ (or ‘Tulasi’ or ‘Thulasi’) and is considered sacred by Hindus. We present the draft genome of <i>Ocimum tenuiflurum</i> L (subtype Krishna Tulsi) in this report. The paired-end and mate-pair sequence libraries were generated for the whole genome sequenced with the Illumina Hiseq 1000, resulting in an assembled genome of 374 Mb, with a genome coverage of 61 % (612 Mb estimated genome size). We have also studied transcriptomes (RNA-Seq) of two subtypes of <i>O. tenuiflorum,</i> Krishna and Rama Tulsi and report the relative expression of genes in both the varieties.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Results</h3><p>The pathways leading to the production of medicinally-important specialized metabolites have been studied in detail, in relation to similar pathways in <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> and other plants. Expression levels of anthocyanin biosynthesis-related genes in leaf samples of Krishna Tulsi were observed to be relatively high, explaining the purple colouration of Krishna Tulsi leaves. The expression of six important genes identified from genome data were validated by performing q-RT-PCR in different tissues of five different species, which shows the high extent of urosolic acid-producing genes in young leaves of the Rama subtype<i>.</i> In addition, the presence of eugenol and ursolic acid, implied as potential drugs in the cure of many diseases including cancer was confirmed using mass spectrometry.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Conclusions</h3><p>The availability of the whole genome of <i>O.tenuiflorum</i> and our sequence analysis suggests that small amino acid changes at the functional sites of genes involved in metabolite synthesis pathways confer special medicinal properties to this herb.</p></div></div></section>
                    
    


                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Background"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Background</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Plants of the genus <i>Ocimum</i> belong to the family Lamiaceae (Order Lamiales) and are widely distributed in the tropical, sub-tropical and warm temperate regions of the world [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Paton A, Harley RM, and Harley MM. Ocimum-an overview of relationships and classification. Ocimum Aromatic Plants-Industrial Profiles. Amsterdam: Harwood Academic (1999)" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d28084e1362">1</a>]. These plants are known to produce essential oils comprising of a number of aromatic compounds and Tulsi is rightly known as the “Queen of Herbs” for this reason. In India, these plants are mostly grown at homes for worship and as offerings in temples. Among plants with medicinal value, those belonging to the genus <i>Ocimum</i> are very important aromatic herbs or shrubs.</p><p>The genus <i>Ocimum</i> is highly variable and possesses wide genetic diversity at intra and inter-species levels. Nine species of <i>Ocimum</i> viz., <i>O. teniuflorum</i> L., <i>O. basilicum</i> L., <i>O. gratissimum</i> L., <i>O. kilimandscharicum, O. micranthum</i> L.<i>, O. campechianum</i> L., <i>O. americanum</i> L., <i>O. minimum</i> L., and <i>O. citriodorum</i> L., are found in India, three of which (<i>O. americanum</i> L., <i>O. minimum</i> L., and <i>O. citriodorum</i> L.) are exotic [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Willis JC. A dictionary of the flowering plants and ferns, by J. C. Willis. Cambridge: The University Press; 1919." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d28084e1412">2</a>]. It is difficult to distinguish all these species on the basis of leaf morphology alone (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#Fig1">1</a>). The metabolites (essential oils) of genus <i>Ocimum</i> have been reported to possess antioxidant and antifungal properties and to cure many diseases including bronchitis in Ayurveda, an Indian system of medicine [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Prakash P, Gupta N. Therapeutic uses of Ocimum sanctum Linn (Tulsi) with a note on eugenol and its pharmacological actions: a short review. Indian J Physiol Pharmacol. 2005;49:125–31." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d28084e1421">3</a>]. Plants produce specialized metabolites as part of their defense mechanisms and these metabolites have significant medicinal properties that cure several human diseases. They can be isolated from various parts of the plant, including leaves, flowers, roots, bark, seeds and stem [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Khare CP. Indian medicinal plants: an illustrated dictionary. Springer Science &amp; Business Media; 2007." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d28084e1425">4</a>]. Pharmacological screening and the systematic study of the chemical constituents of plant metabolites provide a basis for developing new drugs. Some of the important metabolites reported from <i>Ocimum</i> species include linalool, linalyl, geraniol, citral, camphor, eugenol, methyleugenol, methyl chavicol, methyl cinnamate, thymol, safrol, taxol, urosolic acid etc. [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Khare CP. Indian medicinal plants: an illustrated dictionary. Springer Science &amp; Business Media; 2007." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d28084e1431">4</a>]. These metabolites are of immense value in the pharmaceutical, perfume and cosmetic industries. Metabolites derived from <i>Ocimum</i> species have been found to contain many medicinally relevant properties including anti-cancer, antioxidant, antifungal and anti-inflammatory virtues, and are also recommended for the treatment of malaria, bronchitis, diarrhea, dysentery, etc. [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Rao PS, Satelli A, Moridani M, Jenkins M, Rao US. Luteolin induces apoptosis in multidrug resistant cancer cells without affecting the drug transporter function: involvement of cell line-specific apoptotic mechanisms. Int J Cancer. 2012;130:2703–14." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d28084e1437">5</a>]. Essential oils produced as specialized metabolites found in leaves, seeds, flowers and roots of <i>Ocimum</i> species are used in pharmaceutics and many systems of traditional Indian medicine [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Prakash P, Gupta N. Therapeutic uses of Ocimum sanctum Linn (Tulsi) with a note on eugenol and its pharmacological actions: a short review. Indian J Physiol Pharmacol. 2005;49:125–31." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d28084e1444">3</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Khare CP. Indian medicinal plants: an illustrated dictionary. Springer Science &amp; Business Media; 2007." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d28084e1447">4</a>]. Genome and transcriptome sequencing of medicinal plants serve as a robust tool for gene discovery and downstream biochemical pathway discovery of medicinally important metabolites [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Góngora-castillo E, Fedewa G, Yeo Y, Chappell J, Dellapenna D, Buell CR. Genomic approaches for interrogating the biochemistry of medicinal plant species. Methods Enzymol. 2012;517:139–59." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d28084e1450">6</a>]. Recently, an abundance of transcripts for biosynthesis of terpenoids in <i>O. sanctum</i> and of phenylpropanoids in <i>O. basilicum</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Rastogi S, Meena S, Bhattacharya A, Ghosh S, Shukla RK, Sangwan NS, et al. De novo sequencing and comparative analysis of holy and sweet basil transcriptomes. BMC Genomics. 2014;15:588." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d28084e1459">7</a>] was reported during an attempt to compare transcriptomes of the two species of <i>Ocimum</i>. Despite its important role in traditional Indian medicine and its impressive arsenal of bioactive compounds, our understanding of Krishna Tulsi biology is limited. In this paper, we present the draft genome sequence of the non-model plant <i>O. tenuiflorum</i> (subtype Krishna), along with transcriptomes of two subtypes, Krishna and Rama Tulsi from leaf samples. We have identified a large set of genes involved in the production of specialized metabolites of medicinal interest such as apigenin, luteolin, rosmarinic acid pathway, eugenol, and ursolic acid.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x/figures/1" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12870-015-0562-x/MediaObjects/12870_2015_562_Fig1_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12870-015-0562-x/MediaObjects/12870_2015_562_Fig1_HTML.gif" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Plant and leaf morphology of five <i>Ocimum</i> species prevalent in India viz., <i>O. tenuiflorum</i> subtype Krishna, <i>O. tenuiflorum</i> subtype Rama, <i>O. gratissimum</i>, <i>O. sacharicum</i>, <i>O. kilmand.</i> Leaf morphologies are quite different for the five species</p></div></div><div                      </div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome sequencing and assembly of the non-model plant <i>O. tenuiflorum</i> subtype Krishna</h3><p>The paired-end (PE; 2x100-bp) and mate-paired (MP; 2x50-bp) DNA libraries were generated for Krishna Tulsi subtype using Illumina protocols. In total we obtained 373 million reads of PE and 166 million reads of MP data for Krishna Tulsi. Low quality (LQ) sequence reads were trimmed (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S1 and Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S2) and reads with quality scores of less than Q30 were removed. The good quality reads were used for de-novo genome assembly. Median insert size of PE data was 335 (with a median absolute deviation of 21), whereas median insert size of MP data was 2473 (with a median absolute deviation of 704). K-mer 43 was opted as the best assembly from the statistical analysis of different k-mers. We obtained a maximum scaffold length of 184.7 Kb (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#Tab1">1</a>) with an N50 length of 27.1 Kb. This assembly gives rise to a total of 78,224 scaffolds including equal to or more than 100 bp. The current draft assembly of Krishna Tulsi genome is 374.8 Mb in length. The genomic content of Krishna Tulsi is 0.72 pg/2C which is equivalent to 704.6 Mb [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Carović-Stanko K, Liber Z, Besendorfer V, Javornik B, Bohanec B, Kolak I, et al. Genetic relations among basil taxa (Ocimum L.) based on molecular markers, nuclear DNA content, and chromosome number. Plant Syst Evol. 2009;285:13–22." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d28084e1532">8</a>], but the estimated genome size by k-mer method is 612 Mb and 61 % of the estimated genome size was assembled. The genome size reported in the literature [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Carović-Stanko K, Liber Z, Besendorfer V, Javornik B, Bohanec B, Kolak I, et al. Genetic relations among basil taxa (Ocimum L.) based on molecular markers, nuclear DNA content, and chromosome number. Plant Syst Evol. 2009;285:13–22." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d28084e1535">8</a>], may be of a different cultivar. This lower genome coverage may be due to limited sequencing data (only two libraries were used in sequencing) or due to a high percentage of repeats (42.9 %). In terms of depth of sequencing, we sequenced 59× of the genome with paired-end (100 bp) and mate-pair (50 bp) libraries (since one lane can produce approximately 30Gb of data, even assuming that reads cover the entire 612 Mb of the estimated genome size). <i>Ocimum</i> species are characterized by the different basic chromosome numbers x = 8, 10, 12, or 16 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="MORTON JK. Cytotaxonomic studies on the West African Labiatae. J Linn Soc London, Bot. 1962;58:231–83." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d28084e1542">9</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Khosla M, Sobti SN. Karyomorphological Studies in Genus Ocimum II. Sanctum Group. Cytologia 50: 253-263, 1935." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d28084e1545">10</a>]. In case of <i>O. tenuiflorum</i> individuals with 2n = 32, 2n = 36, and 2n = 76 have been recorded and the chromosome number of <i>O. tenuiflorum</i> is observed to be 2n = 36 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Carović-Stanko K, Liber Z, Besendorfer V, Javornik B, Bohanec B, Kolak I, et al. Genetic relations among basil taxa (Ocimum L.) based on molecular markers, nuclear DNA content, and chromosome number. Plant Syst Evol. 2009;285:13–22." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d28084e1554">8</a>].</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Genome assembly results of Krishna Tulsi</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>A comparative analysis of the assemblies generated using PE data alone and with both PE and MP data show that the size and quality of the genome assembled using PE data alone improved substantially with the inclusion of MP data (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#MOESM3">3</a>: Figures S3 and Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#MOESM4">4</a>: Figure S4, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#MOESM5">5</a>: Table S1 and Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#MOESM6">6</a>: Table S2).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Validation of <i>de novo</i> genome assembly, annotation and repeat content of <i>Ocimum tenuiflorum</i> subtype Krishna genome</h3><p>The <i>de novo</i> genome assembly was validated by mapping raw reads to the assembled genome. On an average, 74 % of reads were mapped back to the assembled genome. Almost 83.3 % of the RNA-seq reads were mapped to the assembled genome. The completeness of <i>de novo</i> genome assembly and annotations were also checked with two other approaches i.e., by using CEGMA (Core Eukaryotic Genes Mapping approach) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Parra G, Bradnam K, Korf I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics. 2007;23:1061–7." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d28084e1767">11</a>] and DEG (Database of Essential Genes) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Zhang R, Ou H-Y, Zhang C-T. DEG: a database of essential genes. Nucleic Acids Res. 2004;32(Database issue):D271–2." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d28084e1770">12</a>] (please see Methods for details). First, we searched for essential eukaryotic genes in the <i>O. tenuiflorum</i> assembly. This resulted in the mapping of 85.1 % of complete core proteins (CEGMA) and more than 95 % including partial genes against our genome assembly (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#MOESM7">7</a>: Table S3). Secondly, we searched for the predicted genes from the final assembly of essential genes recorded in the DEG database. We observed that about 89 % of essential genes were included within the assembly. These genes were also validated using Pfam domain annotation and were of comparable domain lengths as the classical members of that family (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#MOESM8">8</a>: Table S4). Phylogenetic trees for highly conserved essential genes like glyceraldehyde 3-phosphate dehydrogenase (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#MOESM9">9</a>: Figure S5), cytochrome P450 (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#MOESM10">10</a>: Figure S6) and actin (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#MOESM11">11</a>: Figure S7) from Krishna Tulsi and their respective homologues were analyzed and compared with other plant species. Krishna Tulsi genes were found to cluster with genes belonging to related species namely, <i>Solanum lycopersicum, Cucumis sativus</i> and even with distantly related <i>Arabidopsis thaliana,</i> indicating that highly conserved genes, essential to plant growth and functioning, have been detected in <i>O. tenuiflorum</i> assemblies. These trends further support the quality of the genome assembly.</p><p>Regarding the repeat content of the genome, we identified 78224 repeat regions, with a GC content of 36.1 %, adding to 160889218 bp (160 Mb), which constituted 42.9 % of assembled genome which is 374806882 bp (374 Mb) long (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#MOESM12">12</a>: Table S5). Long terminal repeats (LTRs) are found in large numbers in plant genomes (Schmidt T<b>,</b> 1999) and a similar trend is also found in the type of repeats identified in the Tulsi genome.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Genome annotation</h3><p>We identified 36768 putative gene models in the initial genome draft (version 1.2) of <i>O. tenuiflorum</i> genome. At least one gene was observed in each of the 10012 scaffolds, with an average of three to four genes per scaffold. During the process of refined gene prediction, 16384 gene models were observed to have expression evidence (RNA-Seq data from leaves of Tulsi (Krishna and Rama)). A total of 19384 gene models have been identified by <i>ab initio</i> means (without any RNA or protein evidence) (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#Tab2">2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 Genome annotation results of Krishna Tulsi</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>All the gene predictions, with or without RNA/protein evidences, were screened based on length (&gt;100 bp). In case of sequential overlaps between different gene models, the gene models which are of longer length and with RNA or protein evidence for a given region of scaffold were preferred over the ones without any evidence.</p><p>There are 31,020 genes with at least one homologue in NRDB and 24,607 genes which contain at least one Pfam domain. In total, 3929 unique Pfam domains were identified for all the predicted genes in Tulsi (please see URL: <a href="http://caps.ncbs.res.in/Ote">http://caps.ncbs.res.in/Ote</a> for the full list of predicted genes). A majority of domains identified were protein kinases or LRR-containing domains (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#MOESM13">13</a>: Figure S8). Further comparison of Pfam results, with assembled plant genomes of similar size, reveals that the number of predicted gene models is in overall agreement in numbers as well as gene boundaries.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Orthology of Tulsi genes</h3><p>The orthology relationships were deduced between Krishna Tulsi (<i>O. tenuiflorum</i>; Ote) and four other species viz. <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> (Ath), <i>Mimulus guttatus</i> (Mgu), <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i> (Sly) and <i>Oryza sativa</i> (Osa) (please see Methods for details). We observe 8370 clusters which contain a total of 89922 gene products from the five plant species (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#Fig2">2a</a>). <i>M. guttatus</i> and <i>O. tenuiflorum</i> share the same order (Lamiales), but belong to different families (Phrymaceae and Lamiaceae, respectively), which was evident from the presence of the highest number of common gene families (11707) between them. This was followed by <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i> (11022), <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> (10206) and <i>Oryza sativa</i> (9154) as expected from taxonomic hierarchy (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#Fig2">2a</a>). We found 17584 genes to be orthologous to any of the above four species. Considering all the 36768 Ote genes, 1282 groups contained only Ote Krishna Tulsi genes (3302). We obtained 16 Ote genes which lack traceable orthology to 22 other plant species and homology relationships (list of these genes is available on the database). Few of these unique Ote genes are transposons.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12870-015-0562-x/MediaObjects/12870_2015_562_Fig2_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12870-015-0562-x/MediaObjects/12870_2015_562_Fig2_HTML.gif" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Distribution and clustering of orthologous genes of Tulsi genome to other related plant genomes. <b>a</b>. Distribution of gene families among five plant genomes. <i>Ocimum tenuiflorum</i> (Ote - green), <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> (Ath – black rectangle), <i>Oryza sativa</i> (Osa – red), <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i> (Sly – blue) and <i>Mimulus guttatus</i> (Mgu – black circle). The numbers in the Venn diagram represent shared and unique gene families across these 5 species obtained by OrthoMCL. <b>b</b>. Horizontal stacked bar plot of all the genes in 23 different genomes. This figure shows ortholog group distribution in all 23 plant species including Tulsi. Each row represents a plant species - <i>Physcomitrella patens</i> (Ppa), <i>Selaginella moellendorffii</i> (Smo)<i>, Oryza sativa</i> (Osa)<i>, Setaria italic</i> (Sit)<i>, Zea mays</i> (Zma)<i>, Sorghum bicolor</i> (Sbi)<i>, Aquilegia caerulea</i> (Aca)<i>, Ocimum tenuiflorum</i> (Ote)<i>, Mimulus guttatus</i> (Mgu)<i>, Solanum lycopersicum</i> (Sly)<i>, Solanum tuberosum</i> (Stu)<i>, Vitis vinifera</i> (Vvi)<i>, Eucalyptus grandis</i> (Egr)<i>, Citrus sinensis</i> (Csi)<i>, Theobroma cacao</i> (Tca)<i>, Carica papaya</i> (Cpa)<i>, Brassica rapa</i> (Bra)<i>, Arabidopsis thaliana</i> (Ath)<i>, Fragaria vesca</i> (Fve)<i>, Prunus persica</i> (Ppe)<i>, Glycine max</i> (Gma)<i>, Medicago truncatula</i> (Mtr)<i>, Populus trichocarpa</i> (Ptr). The bar graph represents ortholog protein groups for that species subdivided into 22 categories depending on the degree of sharing with the other 22 plant species e.g., category 2 represents the number of orthologous groups that have representatives from the species of interest and from one more species out of the 23 species selected for the study</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>In order to inspect in detail the distribution of the orthologous relationship of <i>Ocimum</i> genes across different species and taxonomic levels, 22 fully-sequenced plant genomes (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#MOESM14">14</a>: Table S6) were considered. The orthologous groups from all 23 species were organized according to the clustering. Three hundred and thirty four clusters of genes are present across all the 23 species chosen for the study. Common genes across all species, comprising of their respective orthologous group, are plotted as a horizontal stacked bar plot (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#Fig2">2b</a>). The pattern of sharing orthologous groups is quite unique to primitive plant genomes (like lycophyte and bryophyte) and monocots. However, the pattern observed in the Tulsi genome is quite similar to that of <i>M. guttatus</i> (Mgu). Interestingly, this pattern is somewhat different for two members of Solanacea, which have more genes shared only in two out of 23 genomes, perhaps due to other features such as polyploidy.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Genes involved in synthesis of specialized metabolites of medicinal value: comparative analysis between <i>O. tenuiflorum</i> (Ote, Krishna Tulsi) and other plant genomes</h3><p>Next, we performed a restricted analysis of the genes involved in the metabolite production in Ote and the genomes of a few plant species that are either closely-related (<i>S. lycopersicum</i>, <i>V. vinifera)</i> or well-characterised (<i>M. truncatula</i>, and <i>A. thaliana</i>). We observed 121 (72.45 %), 130 (77.84 %), 106 (63.47 %) and 94 (56.28 %) scaffolds and contigs from the selected four representative genomes associated with 167 metabolite-related scaffolds and contigs in Ote Krishna Tulsi (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#Fig3">3</a>) respectively<i>.</i> In terms of the number of orthologous genes from this selected plant genome associated with metabolite genes of Ote, we observed a similar trend of association as 601, 620, 570 and 556 genes in <i>S. lycopersicum</i>, <i>V. vinifera, M. truncatula</i>, and <i>A. thaliana</i> respectively. These numbers agree with the taxonomic phylogeny and hierarchy, suggesting that the evolution of genes involved in metabolic pathways is not a cause of recent expansions or sudden drifts.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12870-015-0562-x/MediaObjects/12870_2015_562_Fig3_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12870-015-0562-x/MediaObjects/12870_2015_562_Fig3_HTML.gif" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Phylogenetic representation of five selected plant genomes viz., <i>Solanum lycopercicum</i> (72.45 %), <i>Vitis vinifera</i> (77.84 %), <i>Medicago trucatula</i> (63.47 %), and <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> (56.28 %). The numbers indicate percentage of association of these genomes with the metabolite genes of <i>Ocimum</i> genome. These percentages agree with the taxonomic phylogeny and hierarchy, suggesting that the evolution of genes involved in metabolic pathways is not a cause of recent expansions or sudden genome drifts. Height of orange columns in outermost circle represents amount of repeats in corresponding scaffolds</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>When compared against 11,389 scaffolds (greater than 10Kb in size) from Ote, 10032, 9997, 8648 and 8277 scaffolds were found to be associated with the four reference plant genomes (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#MOESM15">15</a>: Figure S9, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#MOESM16">16</a>: Figure S10 and Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#MOESM17">17</a>: Figure S11 for three genomes and Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#MOESM18">18</a>: Table S7 for four genomes). Further, most of the metabolite-related scaffolds in Ote Krishna Tulsi were associated with chromosomes 1, 6, 8, and 10 of tomato (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#Fig4">4</a>). In particular, gene products that are likely associated in luteolin synthesis pathway are observed to cluster in scaffolds, which are similar to nucleotide stretches in Chromosomes 3, 5, 6, 8 and 10 of the tomato genome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#Fig4">4</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Fig. 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12870-015-0562-x/MediaObjects/12870_2015_562_Fig4_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12870-015-0562-x/MediaObjects/12870_2015_562_Fig4_HTML.gif" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Circular representation of <i>O.tenuiflorum</i> metabolite related genes mapped onto chromosomes of <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i> genome. Height of orange column <img src="//media.springernature.com/full/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12870-015-0562-x/MediaObjects/12870_2015_562_Fige_HTML.gif" alt="" /> in outer circle represents amount of repeats present in respective scaffold.                         <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Transcriptome <i>de novo</i> assembly of Krishna and Rama Tulsi mature leaf samples</h3><p>
                           <i>De novo</i> transcriptome assembly was performed for the mature leaf samples of subtype Krishna Tulsi. The best assembly resulted in 109291 contigs with N50 of 893 bp and longest sequence of 12.1 Kb. All these contigs added up to 49.5 Mb with a GC content of 42.9 %. Scaffolding of these contigs resulted in 89878 scaffolds with N50 of 1597 bp and longest sequence of 12.7 Kb. All these scaffolds added up to 56.3 Mb with a GC content of 42.9 % (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#Tab3">3</a>). Similarly, assembly was performed for the subtype Rama Tulsi and combined reads (Krishna and Rama Tulsi) as well (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#Tab3">3</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-3"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab3" data-test="table-caption">Table 3 Transcriptome assembly of <i>Ocimum tenuiflorum</i> subtype Krishna, Rama and combined data</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x/tables/3"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Differential expression of transcripts</h3><p>The differentially expressed genes found in the transcriptomes of both the Tulsi subtypes were analysed. We observe a substantial number of genes up-regulated and down-regulated in Krishna Tulsi, compared to Rama Tulsi. Some of the highly expressed genes were also confirmed by q-RT-PCR technique in different tissue samples i.e., stems, leaves and flowers and also in five species viz. <i>O. tenuiflorum</i> subtype Krishna and Rama, <i>O. gratissimum</i>, <i>O. basilicum</i>, and <i>O. kilmand.</i>
                        </p><p>For a comparison, we generated a heat map of the top 50 differentially more abundant genes in Krishna Tulsi samples (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#Fig5">5a</a>). Similarly, top 50 differentially more abundant genes in Rama with respect to Krishna sample were also plotted (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#Fig5">5b</a>). Gamma-cadinene synthase is one of the top 50 differentially expressed transcripts with RPKM values of 577.0 and 31.7 in Krishna and Rama Tulsi samples, respectively (please see below for details). Other highly expressed transcripts in Krishna Tulsi sample are Heat shock cognate protein 80, Cellulose synthase A catalyic subunit 6 (UDP-forming), Fructose-biphosphate aldolase (chloroplatic), Phototropin-2, and Rubisco activase 1 (chloroplatic). The chalcone synthase or naringenin-chalcone synthase (CHS) is one of the enzymes important for coloration of plant parts, which is observed to be highly expressed. Abundance values of all the transcripts, along with their functional annotations by NCBI BLAST results and their corresponding Krishna Tulsi genomic scaffold, show several genes involved in the synthesis of specialized metabolites implicated to be of medicinal value (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#MOESM19">19</a>: Table S8).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Fig. 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12870-015-0562-x/MediaObjects/12870_2015_562_Fig5_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12870-015-0562-x/MediaObjects/12870_2015_562_Fig5_HTML.gif" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Transcript expression of Tulsi Krishna and Rama subtypes are expressed as RPKM values. Highly significant differentially abundant RNA scaffolds/transcripts were defined to have RPKM of atleast 5 in both and the fold-change difference between two subtypes should be atleast 8 times. Only the transcripts, for which the 95 % lower-confidence-bound of more abundant subtype and 95 % upper-confidence-bound of less abundant subtype, and had at least 8 times difference, were retained. Of these differentially abundant transcripts, top-50 in Krishna and Rama subtype were plotted in the form of heat-map. <b>a</b>. Differentially more abundant transcripts in Krishna. <b>b</b>. Differentially more abundant transcripts in Rama. (please look in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#MOESM24">24</a>: Text B and C for transcript IDs for a. and b)</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>Dark purple coloration of the leaves and stem of subtype Krishna Tulsi is one of its characteristic phenotypes, which distinguishes it from other subtypes and species of genus <i>Ocimum.</i> Chalcone synthase (CHS) is an enzyme belonging to a family of polyketide synthases which catalyzes the initial step for flavonoid biosynthesis. Flavonoids are important plant specific metabolites that perform various functions such as pigmentation, antifungal defense <i>etc</i>. Reviewed protein sequence for CHS from UniProt (Universal Protein resource) database [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Apweiler R, Bairoch A, Wu CH, Barker WC, Boeckmann B, Ferro S, et al. UniProt: the Universal Protein knowledgebase. Nucleic Acids Res. 2004;32(Database issue):D115–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d28084e2766">13</a>] was employed to search against annotated protein sequences of Krishna Tulsi genome and six transcripts were obtained as possible hits. The best hit could be identified with 95 % query coverage and 99 % sequence identity. The extent of abundance of this hit (protein sequence) was checked in the leaf transcriptome of both the Tulsi subtypes viz. Krishna and Rama. Abundance (in terms of RPKM) of the six transcripts was, on an average, two times more in case of Krishna as compared to Rama (please see Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#Fig5">5</a>), and may be involved in the coloration phenotype of Krishna subtype plants [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Ferrer JL, Jez JM, Bowman ME, Dixon RA, Noel JP. Structure of chalcone synthase and the molecular basis of plant polyketide biosynthesis. Nat Struct Biol. 1999;6:775–84." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d28084e2772">14</a>]. For further confirmation of expression of these transcripts, q-RT-PCR was performed. As expected, anthocyanin producing gene was observed to be more abundant in Krishna young leaf samples and mature leaf samples (used as control) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#Fig6">6a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#Fig6">b</a>). In contrast, the chlorophyll binding protein was more abundant in Krishna mature leaf samples. In addition, we also examined the presence of gamma-cadeninene synthase gene which is responsible for aroma [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Portnoy V, Benyamini Y, Bar E, Harel-Beja R, Gepstein S, Giovannoni JJ, et al. The molecular and biochemical basis for varietal variation in sesquiterpene content in melon (Cucumis melo L.) rinds. Plant Mol Biol. 2008;66:647–61." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d28084e2782">15</a>]. This gene was found to be more abundant in Rama root sample and young leaf samples of <i>O. Saccharum</i>, but not observed in higher quantities in <i>O. kilmund</i>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Fig. 6"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 6</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12870-015-0562-x/MediaObjects/12870_2015_562_Fig6_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12870-015-0562-x/MediaObjects/12870_2015_562_Fig6_HTML.gif" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>Expression quantification of selected genes by q-RT-PCR method. <b>a</b>. Fold changes of genes involved in color production, obtained through q-RT PCR. Blue colour horizontal bar is for chlorophyll a-b binding protein, red to denote Gamma-cadenine synthase and green to denote Anthocyanin. Mature leaf of Krishna subtype was used as control. It can be seen that, genes responsible for color production such as Chlorophyll a-b binding protein and gene in anthacyanin pathway are down-regulated as compared to mature Krishna leaf, which corresponds to phenotypic characteristics. <b>b</b>. Fold changes of genes involved in ursolic acid biosynthetic pathway, as obtained through qRT-PCR for 5 different Tulsi subtypes. Blue colour horizontal bar is for squalene epoxidase, red to denote alpha-amyrin synthase and green to denote Cytochrome P450 monooxygenase. Mature leaf of Krishna subtype was used as control. Mature leaf of Rama subtype has high expression of genes while expression in <i>Ocimum kilmund</i> is low. Expression of these genes are uniformly high in small, developing plants. Samples are as follows: 1) <i>O. tenuiflorum</i> (Rama) - Sampling Leaf. 2) <i>O. tenuiflorum</i> (Rama) - Sampling Root. 3) <i>O. tenuiflorum</i> (Rama) - Mature Leaf. 4) <i>O. tenuiflorum</i> (Krishna) - Sampling Leaf. 5) <i>O. tenuiflorum</i> (Krishna) - Sampling Root. 6) <i>O. gratissimum</i> - Sampling Leaf. 7) <i>O. gratissimum</i> - Sampling Root. 8) <i>O. gratissimum</i> - Mature Leaf. 9) <i>O. sacharicum</i> - Sampling Leaf. 10) <i>O. sacharicum</i> - Sampling Root. 11) <i>O. sacharicum</i> - Mature Leaf. 12) <i>O. kilmund</i> - Sampling Leaf. 13) <i>O. kilmund</i> - Sampling Root. 14) <i>O. kilmund</i> - Mature Leaf</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Specialized metabolites detection and validation</h3><p>Nearly 30 specialized metabolites (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#Fig7">7a</a>) are reported form the genus <i>Ocimum</i> which are found to have medicinal values or properties [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Khare CP. Indian medicinal plants: an illustrated dictionary. Springer Science &amp; Business Media; 2007." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d28084e2877">4</a>]. Amongst these, 14 metabolites belonging to five basic groups were found to have complete pathway information in PlantCyc database (<a href="http://www.plantcyc.org/">http://www.plantcyc.org/</a>) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Plant Metabolic Network (PMN), &#xA;                    http://www.plantcyc.org/tools/tools_overview.faces&#xA;                    &#xA;                   on www.plantcyc.org, February 28, 2008. [http://www.plantcyc.org/about/citing_pmn.faces]. Date Accessed 05-08-2014." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d28084e2887">16</a>] (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#MOESM20">20</a>: Figure S12). Hence, genes involved in these pathways were chosen for further analysis and searched against the assembled genome of <i>O. tenuiflorum</i>. Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#Fig7">7b</a> highlights the distribution of the genes identified in various classes of metabolites of disease relevance (i.e., these metabolites are well-known as drugs in the cure of human diseases).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-7" data-title="Fig. 7"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig7" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 7</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x/figures/7" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12870-015-0562-x/MediaObjects/12870_2015_562_Fig7_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-7-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12870-015-0562-x/MediaObjects/12870_2015_562_Fig7_HTML.gif" alt="figure7" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-7-desc"><p>Number of genes involved in specialized metabolite synthesis in Tulsi genome. <b>a</b>. Number in the bracket is percentage of sepecialized metabolites present in the genome. 458 genes were identified as coding for enzymes involved in synthesis of specialized metabolites. <b>b</b>.
