The enzymes have been labelled with 5–7 letters for convenience. The numbers after the’_’ in the enzyme label represent the number of putative hits found for the given enzyme in the genomic assembly of <i>O. tenuiflorum</i>. The metabolites involved in the disease relevance and the enzymes involved in the synthesis of these metabolites are as follows: APIGENIN (Flavone-synthaseI-FSYN1, Naringenin-NADPH-oxygen-oxidoreductase-NNOOX), LUTEOLIN (Flavone-synthaseI-FSYN1, Naringenin-NADPH-oxygen-oxidoreductase-NNOOX, Flavone-3-monooxygenase-F3MON), TAXOL (Taxadiene-synthase-TSYN, Taxadiene-5-alpha-hydroxylase-T5AHYD, Taxadien-5-alpha-ol-O-acetyltransferase-T5AOOA, Taxane-10-beta-hydroxylase-T10BHYD, Taxoid-14-beta-hydroxylase-T14BHYD, 2-alpha-hydroxytaxane-2-O-benzoyltransferase-2AH2OB, 10-deacetylbaccatin-III-10-O-acetyltransferase-10D10OA, 3-N-debenzoyl-2-deoxytaxol-N-benzoyltransferase-3ND2DNB, URSOLIC ACID (ursolic-aldehyde-28-monooxygenase-UA28M, Alpha-amyrin-synthase-AASYN), OLEANOLIC ACID (Beta-amyrin-synthase-BASYN, oleanolic-aldehyde-28-monooxygenase-OA28M), SITOSTEROL (24C-methyltransferase-24CMET), ROSMARINIC ACID I (4-coumaroyl-4-hydroxyphenyllactate-3-hydroxylase-4C4H3H, Tyrosine-transaminase-TTRAN), ROMARINIC ACID II (Hydroxyphenylpyruvate-reductase-HPPRE, Tyrosine-3-monooxygenase-TTRAN), METHYL CAHVICOL (Eugenol-o-methyltransferase-EOMET), EUGENOL (Alcohol-o-acetyltransferase-AOACE, Eugenol-synthase-ESYN, Isoeugenol-synthase-ISYN), LINALOOL (Farnesyl-pyrophosphate-synthase-FPSYN, R-linool-synthase-RLSYN, S-linool-synthase-SLSYN), CARYOPHYLENE (Alpha-humulene-synthase-AHSYN, Beta-caryophyllene-synthase-BCSYN), SELINENE (Alpha-selinene-synthase-ASSYN, Beta-selinene-synthase-BSSYN), CITRAL (Geraniol-synthase-GSYN, Geraniol-dehdrogenase-GDHYD)</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x/figures/7" data-track-dest="link:Figure7 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>A total of 458 genes were identified in Ote genome, which are either homologous or directly code for enzymes involved in the synthesis of specialized metabolites (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#Fig8">8</a>) (details of gene IDs of these proteins are provided in Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#Tab4">4</a> and Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#MOESM21">21</a>: Table S9). Twenty eight <i>O. tenuiflorum</i> gene products were annotated as putative terpene synthases using BLAST sequence searches with E-value of 10<sup>−4</sup> and query coverage filter of &gt;75 % (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#MOESM22">22</a>: Table S10).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-8" data-title="Fig. 8"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig8" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 8</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x/figures/8" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12870-015-0562-x/MediaObjects/12870_2015_562_Fig8_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-8-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12870-015-0562-x/MediaObjects/12870_2015_562_Fig8_HTML.gif" alt="figure8" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-8-desc"><p>Phylogeny of terpene synthases of representative sequences of six classes from the plant kingdom along with putative Tulsi terpene synthases genes: The tree is color coded as tpsa:red, tbsb:blue, tpsc:yellow, tpsd: green, tpse: blue and tpsf:purple</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x/figures/8" data-track-dest="link:Figure8 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                           <div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-4"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab4" data-test="table-caption">Table 4 The enzymes involved in metabolite biosynthesis were identified in the assembled genome and these genes were analyzed for their expression level in the transcriptome. The RKPM value signifies the level for expression</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x/tables/4"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>Among these specialized metabolites, we focused on ursolic acid, belonging to sesquiterpenes, since it is known to have anti-inflammatory, anti-microbial, anti-tumour and anti-cancer properties. The synthesis of ursolic acid from squalene is a three-step process starting from squalene (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#Fig9">9</a>). α-Amyrin is formed by concerted cyclization of squalene epoxide, while ursolic acid is eventually synthesized by the catalytic activity of multifunctional cytochrome P450. The enzymes involved are, therefore, squalene epoxidase, alpha-amyrin synthase and alpha-amyrin 2, 8 monoxygenase. Sequence search algorithms were employed to search for the three enzymes of this pathway in the Tulsi genome, starting from protein sequences for each of these enzymes from PlantCyc database as queries. The search for squalene epoxidase in Tulsi, using the sequence of this enzyme in <i>Oryza sativa japonica</i> (LOC_Os02g04710.2) as a query, gave rise to a hit (C3776143), with 50 % sequence identity covering 80 % of the query length (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#MOESM23">23</a>: Figure S13). Using Amyrin synthase LUP2 from <i>A. thaliana</i> (Q8RWT0) and 13 other well-accepted alpha/beta amyrin synthases as a query, four hits were identified in the Tulsi genome (scaffold16333, scaffold20801, scaffold12312 and maker-C3776143). In classical amyrin synthases, a QW structural motif repeats six times in the entire sequence [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Brendolise C, Yauk Y-K, Eberhard ED, Wang M, Chagne D, Andre C, et al. An unusual plant triterpene synthase with predominant α-amyrin-producing activity identified by characterizing oxidosqualene cyclases from Malus × domestica. FEBS J. 2011;278:2485–99." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d28084e4224">17</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Poralla K, Hewelt A, Prestwich GD, Abe I, Reipen I, Sprenger G. A specific amino acid repeat in squalene and oxidosqualene cyclases. Trends Biochem Sci. 1994;19:157–8." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d28084e4228">18</a>], while there are two functional motifs, <i>viz</i>., a well conserved SDTAE [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Abe I, Prestwich GD. Identification of the active site of vertebrate oxidosqualene cyclase. Lipids. 1995;30:231–4." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d28084e4234">19</a>] motif which is believed to form the catalytic pocket and the MWCYCR [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Kushiro T, Shibuya M, Masuda K, Ebizuka Y. Mutational studies on triterpene synthases: engineering lupeol synthase into -amyrin synthase. 2000:6816–6824." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d28084e4237">20</a>] motif that is shown to play a crucial role in catalysis. These motifs are observed in the four hits in Tulsi genome (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#MOESM24">24</a>: Text D). Further, a phylogenetic tree was constructed using 16 query sequences and these four hits (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#Fig10">10</a>). One of the Tulsi hits, (scaffold 16333_mrnal) clusters with a well-characterized alpha amyrin synthase from <i>C. roseus</i> (H2ER439) suggesting that this particular scaffold might indeed retain an alpha amyrin synthase.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-9" data-title="Fig. 9"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig9" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 9</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x/figures/9" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12870-015-0562-x/MediaObjects/12870_2015_562_Fig9_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-9-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12870-015-0562-x/MediaObjects/12870_2015_562_Fig9_HTML.gif" alt="figure9" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-9-desc"><p>The synthesis of ursolic acid from squalene is a three-step process starting from squalene. A: Squalene epoxidase, B: α-amyrin synthase, C1: α-amyrin 28-monooxygenase [Multifunctional], C2: Uvaol dehydrogenase [Multifunctional] and C3: Ursolic aldehyde 28-monooxygenase. Squalene epoxidase and alpha amyrin synthase, along with alpha amyrin 28 mono-oxygenase, uvol dehydrogenase and ursolic aldehyde 28 mono-oxygenase, play important role in synthesis of ursolic acid. These three genes have been chosen for quantification of gene expression by q-RT PCR method in different tissues and species</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x/figures/9" data-track-dest="link:Figure9 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                           <div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-10" data-title="Fig. 10"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig10" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 10</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x/figures/10" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12870-015-0562-x/MediaObjects/12870_2015_562_Fig10_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-10-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12870-015-0562-x/MediaObjects/12870_2015_562_Fig10_HTML.gif" alt="figure10" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-10-desc"><p>Phylogenetic tree of sixteen amyrin query sequences and four putative amyrins from Tulsi. Tulsi hits are marked in blue clour, red ones are alpha amyrin synthase, greens are beta amyrin synthase and cyan ones are proteins from other class of amyrin. The presence of motifs and position in the phylogeny indicate that the hits obtained in <i>O. tenuiflorum</i> genome are likely to be alpha-amyrin synthases</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x/figures/10" data-track-dest="link:Figure10 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>Interestingly, many genes involved in the synthesis of specialized metabolites of relevance in the treatment of diseases are also more abundant, as observed in the assembled transcriptome (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#MOESM21">21</a>: Table S9). Similarly, genes involved in the synthesis of 16 other specialized metabolites (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#MOESM25">25</a>: Table S11), are also equally interesting. However, this requires detailed understanding of the mechanism of synthesis and enzymes involved in the pathways. We analysed RNA-Seq data of two leaf samples in order to compare the genes related to important metabolite pathways and the peculiar phenotype of <i>O. tenuiflorum</i> subtype Krishna with subtype Rama Tulsi. There were 104 transcripts, whose fold change in expression was observed to be eight times more in Krishna Tulsi than in Rama Tulsi. Likewise, there were 229 transcripts whose fold change expression was eight times more in Rama Tulsi as compared to Krishna Tulsi. These are available for download at- (caps.ncbs.res.in/download/tdat_data/Supplementary_tables/Supplementary Table 8.txt).</p><p>In the case of the multifunctional Cytochome P450 (which catalyses the last three steps in the synthesis of urosolic acid, Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#Fig9">9</a>), a predicted gene from scaffold2032 was obtained as a hit, when a reviewed UniProt entry F1T282 from <i>V. vinifera</i> was considered as query and searched in the Tulsi genome assembly using BLAST. This hit retains 61 % sequence identity and the alignment covers 90 % of the length of the query (alignments are shown in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#MOESM23">23</a>: Figure S13). This scaffold contains a total of three predicted genes viz., Ote100020320011, Ote100020320001 (similar to UHRF1-binding protein) and Ote100020320031 (gene of interest).</p><p>From the available transcriptome assembly, these genes, identified as involved in the synthesis of urosolic acid, were analysed for their levels of expression. The RPKM values were also high for these three genes (please see Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#MOESM21">21</a>: Table S9). To further validate the levels of expression of these genes, q-RT-PCR was performed using sequence-specific primers. The presence of these three enzymes is generally high in all the mature leaf samples and highest in Rama subtype (using Krishna subtype as control). Alpha-amyrin synthase is more abundant in mature leaf samples of <i>O. gratissimum</i> and <i>O. sacharicum</i> species. However, interestingly, the three enzymes are found to be more abundant in the young leaf samples of Rama subtype; in contrast, atleast one of the three genes is less in the Krishna leaf sample and in all root samples. The expression of the three genes implicated in urosolic acid synthesis is uniformly low in samples of <i>O. kilmund.</i>
                        </p><p>Next, to correlate gene expression and to quantify the presence of ursolic acid and eugenol, chemical profiling was performed using LC-Mass spectrometry from different tissues and samples. Eugenol and ursolic acid were observed in the highest quantities in mature leaf sample of Rama subtype and in relatively low quantities in <i>O. kilmund</i>. The amount of eugenol in the leaf sample of <i>O. tenuiflorum</i> subtype Rama (2235 ng/mg) is considerably high followed by <i>O. kilmund</i> (1472 ng/mg), <i>O. sacharicum</i> (651 ng/mg) and lowest in <i>O. gratissimum</i> (73 ng/mg). In all stem samples, the amount of eugenol is consistently low with the highest in <i>O. tenuiflorum</i> subtype Rama (24 ng/mg), <i>O. tenuiflorum</i> subtype Krishna (17 ng/mg), <i>O. kilmund</i> (15 ng/mg) and below limits of quantification in <i>O. gratissimum</i> and <i>O. sacharicum</i>. The presence of oleanolic acid is also severely reduced in stem samples of Rama subtype (2869 ng/mg) and in Krishna subtype (1088 ng/mg) in comparison to the mature leaf samples (7556 ng/mg for Rama and 4630 ng/mg for Krishna). The presence of urosolic acid is 50 % less in stem samples of Rama subtype (2883 ng/mg) when compared to the mature leaf samples (4597), whereas it is much lower in the stem samples of other species as compared to the leaf sample. The amount of ursolic acid in the stem samples of Krishna subtype (746 ng/mg) is 4.6 times less than that of the mature leaf samples (3471 ng/mg) (please see Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#Tab5">5</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-5"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab5" data-test="table-caption">Table 5 Estimation results of Eugenol and Ursolic acid in different samples</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x/tables/5"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        </div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><p>
                        <i>O. tenuiflorum</i> subtype Krishna Tulsi is one of the non-model plants of great medicinal value, for which there has been no genomic information available till date. We have performed genome sequencing of <i>O. tenuiflorum</i> subtype Krishna of the paired-end (PE; 2x100-bp) and mate-paired (MP; 2x50-bp) DNA libraries by Illumina Hiseq 1000. The best <i>de novo</i> assembly was obtained at k-mer 43 by SOAPdenovo2, an eukaryotic <i>de novo</i> genome assembler. Repeats were identified and masked, and gene prediction and annotation was carried out using the MAKER annotation pipeline by using genomic, transcriptomics and EST data. The nearest species whose genome has been sequenced is the monkey flower (<i>M. guttatus</i>), which shares its order Lamiales with <i>O. tenuiflorum</i> (Ote) but falls in a different family (Phrymaceae). Orthology search of Ote Krishna Tulsi genes in four genomes viz. <i>A. thaliana</i> (Ath), <i>M. guttatus</i> (Mgu), <i>S. lycopersicum</i> (Sly) and <i>O. sativa</i> (Osa) also confirmed the close relationship between Krishna Tulsi and <i>M. guttatus</i> (Mgu), in terms of the number of common gene families i.e., 578 out of 2488 total genes. When we considered all the 36,768 predicted genes from the Krishna Tulsi genome, we found that 1282 ortholog groups have <i>Ocimum-</i>only genes. These 1282 groups contain 13,306 <i>Ocimum</i> genes and hence they are referred to as paralogs by OrthoMCL. Of the remaining Ote genes, 17,584 genes were found to be orthologous to any of the other four species studied in this case. We performed an analysis of the genes involved in the metabolite production in <i>Ote</i> and the genomes of a few other related plant species. Based on the direct evidence or homology a total of 458 genes were identified in Ote genome, which are involved in coding of enzymes implied in the synthesis of specialized metabolites. Comparative analysis of transciptomes of <i>O. tenuiflorum</i> subtype Krishna and Rama was performed to detect potential differentially-regulated genes and their involvement in metabolite synthesis. On comparing both the transcriptomes, differentially expressed genes were observed with a substantial number of genes more abundant and others less abundant in either subtypes. Gamma-cadinene synthase is more abundant in Krishna sample (RPKM value 577.047) as compared to Rama sample (RPKM value 31.73). To confirm some of the more abundant genes along with Gamma-cadinene synthase, we performed q-RT-PCR in different tissue samples i.e., stem and leaves and also in five species viz. <i>O. tenuiflorum</i> subtype Krishna and Rama, <i>O. gratissimum</i>, <i>O. basilicum</i>, and <i>O. kilmand.</i> Expression of Gamma-cadinene synthase is found more in Krishna samples as compared to Rama by q-RT-PCR also. Likewise, Chalcone synthase (CHS) is an anthocyanin-producing gene, which is observed to be more abundant in young leaf samples of Krishna and mature leaf samples in transcriptome data. Subsequently, this has been confirmed by q-RT-PCR and from mass spectrometry readings of ursolic acid and eugenol from different tissue samples and from different species.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec12" data-title="Conclusion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec12-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec12">Conclusion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec12-content"><p>We present a draft genome of <i>O. tenuiflorum</i> Krishna Tulsi subtype Krishna Tulsi. The habitat of genus <i>Ocimum</i> is tropical climate and it is wide spread over Asia, Africa, Central and South America. High RNA-seq expression values of the genes responsible for the purple coloration of the plant parts in Krishna subtype, as compared to Rama subtype, were observed. We also identified a fFew unique genes (16) of Ote, which lack any traceable orthology and homology relationships from all the 22 species used in this study.</p><p>Krishna Tulsi is described in the Vedas and Puranas (ancient scriptures of Hindus) and has a long history of cultivation, of roughly 3000 years, and is therefore assumed to be of Indian origin [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Bast F, Rani P, Meena D. Chloroplast DNA phylogeography of holy basil (Ocimum tenuiflorum) in Indian subcontinent. ScientificWorldJournal. 2014;2014:847482." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d28084e4770">21</a>]. In literature, it is also referred to as the “Queen of Herbs”. Major genes involved in the synthesis of medicinally important specialized metabolites in the plant could be unraveled despite limited data on sequencing and coverage [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Wang W, Feng B, Xiao J, Xia Z, Zhou X, Li P, et al. Cassava genome from a wild ancestor to cultivated varieties. Nat Commun. 2014;5:5110." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d28084e4773">22</a>]. Expressions of these genes were confirmed by complementing with RNA-seq data and q-RT-PCR method. We also investigated one of the important metabolic pathways involving the production of ursolic acid in detail, by mass-spectrometry and q-RT-PCR methods. Synthesis of specialized metabolites or their precursors appear to begin in the young leaves of Tulsi. Subsequently, the mature leaves retain the medicinally relevant metabolites. <i>O. tenuiflorum</i> Rama subtype retains the high abundance of key medicinally relevant metabolites like eugenol and ursolic acid, as observed in the transcriptome, metabolite quantifications and q-RT-PCR expression values consistent with its high medicinal values. Our main emphasis was to unravel the important metabolite genes by using genomic and transcriptomic data despite limited sequencing information.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec13" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec13-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec13">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec13-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Isolation of genomic DNA from <i>O. tenuiflorum</i> subtype Krishna Tulsi</h3><p>Young leaves of Tulsi subtype Krishna and Rama were used for genomic DNA isolation. About one gram of leaves were crushed using liquid Nitrogen and DNA extraction buffer (200 mM TrisHCL [pH-8.0], 200 mM NaCl, 25 mM EDTA and 1 % PVP) was added [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Ghosh P, Chattopadhyay SK, Adhikari S, Saha S, Mondal S. A high throughput DNA extraction method from chemotypically heterogeneous plant species. P. 2013;2603." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d28084e4795">23</a>]. The ground material along with 1/10th volume of 20 % SDS solution was incubated at 65 °C for 30 min. The tubes were centrifuged at 14,000 RPM for 10 min at room temperature to remove the debris. The supernatant was transferred into a fresh tube and treated with equal volume of phenol: chloroform: isoamyl alcohol (25:24:1) and mixed gently for 5 min. The mixture was centrifuged at 12,000 RPM for 10 min to separate the phases. The aqueous phase from the centrifuged tube was transferred to a fresh tube and DNA was precipitated with 1/5th volume of 2 M NaCl and 2 volumes of ice-cold ethanol. The DNA was pelleted by centrifugation at 12,000 RPM for 10 min. Precipitated DNA pellet was taken as a starting material for purification using the Sigma Genelute plant DNA isolation kit (G2N70, Sigma). The DNA was run on a 1 % agarose gel to assess the quality. The A260/280 ratio and quantity were determined using the nanodrop.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec15">Genome sequencing, assembly and annotation</h3><p>Genome sequencing was performed by using Illumina HiSeq 1000 technology in the Next Generation Genomics Facility at Centre for Cellular and Molecular Platforms (C-CAMP). Genomic DNA paired-end and gel free mate-pair library preparation was performed for Krishna Tulsi using TruSeq DNA sample preparation kit (FC-121-2001) and Nextera mate-pair sample preparation kit (FC-132-1001) from Illumina (<a href="http://www.illumina.com">www.illumina.com</a>). FASTX-Toolkit [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Lab H. FASTX toolkit. &#xA;                    http://hannonlab.cshl.edu/fastx_toolkit/index.html&#xA;                    &#xA;                  ." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d28084e4813">24</a>] and FastQC tools [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Andrews S. FastQC a quality control tool for high throughput sequence data. &#xA;                    http://www.bioinformatics.babraham.ac.uk/projects/fastqc/&#xA;                    &#xA;                  ." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d28084e4816">25</a>] were used for pre-processing of raw reads and for quality check of the reads. Genome assembly from reads of PE and MP together was done by using SOAPdenovo2, a <i>de novo</i> draft genome assembler [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Xie Y, Wu G, Tang J, Luo R, Patterson J, Liu S, et al. SOAPdenovo-Trans: de novo transcriptome assembly with short RNA-Seq reads. Bioinformatics. 2014;30:1660–6." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d28084e4822">26</a>]. Preliminary assemblies were performed based on k-mers from 21 to 63 with an interval of two. Gene prediction and annotation was carried out using the MAKER annotation pipeline [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Cantarel BL, Korf I, Robb SMC, Parra G, Ross E, Moore B, et al. MAKER: an easy-to-use annotation pipeline designed for emerging model organism genomes. Genome Res. 2008;18:188–96." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d28084e4826">27</a>] with predicted gene models using AUGUSTUS [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Stanke M, Morgenstern B. AUGUSTUS: a web server for gene prediction in eukaryotes that allows user-defined constraints. Nucleic Acids Res. 2005;33(Web Server issue):W465–7." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d28084e4829">28</a>] and <i>A. thaliana</i> genes as reference for initial prediction. The gene models were refined using homology searches against all protein sequences from <i>Viridaeplantae</i> kingdom.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec16">Validation of genome assembly and annotations</h3><p>To validate genome assembly, we have mapped raw reads on to the <i>de novo</i> assembled genome by using REAPR (SMALT) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Hunt M, Kikuchi T, Sanders M, Newbold C, Berriman M, Otto TD. REAPR: a universal tool for genome assembly evaluation. Genome Biol. 2013;14:R47." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d28084e4849">29</a>], SAMtools [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Heng L, Bob H, Alec W, Tim F, Jue Ruan NH, Gabor M, et al. The Sequence Alignment/Map format and SAMtools.pdf. Bioinformatics. 2009;25:2078–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d28084e4852">30</a>] and Picard tools (<a href="http://broadinstitute.github.io/picard/">http://broadinstitute.github.io/picard/</a>). Maximum and minimum insert size of 500 bp and 0 bp respectively were selected for mapping. We report an alignment pairing with best score, using standard Smith-Waterman scores. The threshold minimum score used was calculated by the formula to be: &lt; Minimum score &gt; = &lt; world length &gt; + step size – 1. Here the word length of 13 is used with a step size of 6. Estimation of the genome size of the Tulsi genome was done using the k-mer distribution analysis by Jellyfish [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Marçais G, Kingsford C. A fast, lock-free approach for efficient parallel counting of occurrences of k-mers. Bioinformatics. 2011;27:764–70." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d28084e4862">31</a>]. Essential genes implicated in the regulation, assembly and functioning of plant cells, have been identified in the Krishna Tulsi assembled genome using a two-way approach. Firstly, using CEGMA which was derived from the KOG database [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Tatusov RL, Fedorova ND, Jackson JD, Jacobs AR, Kiryutin B, Koonin EV, et al. The COG database: an updated version includes eukaryotes. BMC Bioinformatics. 2003;4:41." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d28084e4866">32</a>] (for eukaryotic genomes) and core proteins in any eukaryotic genome (including ones in draft stages), essential genes were annotated. Secondly, a subset of <i>A. thaliana</i> genes were extracted from a well-characterized Database of Essential Genes (DEG) and compared against Krishna Tulsi assemblies. Validation of the extracted genes was performed by Pfam domain annotation approaches. Putative essential genes from the Krishna Tulsi dataset were further searched using BLASTP [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Altschul SF, Madden TL, Schaffer AA, Zhang J, Zhang Z, Miller W, et al. Gapped BLAST and PSI-BLAST: a new generation of protein database search programs. Nucleic Acids Res. 1997;25:3389–402." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d28084e4872">33</a>] against the NCBI (NR) database and closely-related homologues were aligned and phylogenetic tree constructed.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec17">Repeat identification</h3><p>Repeat elements in the assembled genome were identified using RepeatScout (version 1.0.5) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Price AL, Jones NC, Pevzner PA. De novo identification of repeat families in large genomes. Bioinformatics. 2005;21 Suppl 1:i351–8." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d28084e4883">34</a>] and RepeatMasker (version 4.0.3) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="RepeatMasker open-3.0.;1996–2010. &#xA;                    http://www.repeatmasker.org&#xA;                    &#xA;                  . [&#xA;                    http://www.repeatmasker.org/faq.html#faq3&#xA;                    &#xA;                  ]" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d28084e4886">35</a>]. The library of <i>ab initio</i> repeats generated by RepeatScout was classified into known repeat classes using the RepeatClassifier module of RepeatScout (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#MOESM12">12</a>: Table S5). The RepBase library of RepeatMasker and the non-redundant library of <i>ab-initio</i> classified repeats were then used to mask the repeat elements in the assembled genome. The repeat-masked genome assembly was then used for genome annotation.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec18">Genome annotation</h3><p>The repeat-masked assembled genome of Krishna Tulsi was processed through the MAKER annotation pipeline [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Cantarel BL, Korf I, Robb SMC, Parra G, Ross E, Moore B, et al. MAKER: an easy-to-use annotation pipeline designed for emerging model organism genomes. Genome Res. 2008;18:188–96." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d28084e4907">27</a>]. AUGUSTUS [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Stanke M, Morgenstern B. AUGUSTUS: a web server for gene prediction in eukaryotes that allows user-defined constraints. Nucleic Acids Res. 2005;33(Web Server issue):W465–7." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d28084e4910">28</a>] was used for gene prediction, trained on <i>A. thaliana</i> gene models. RNA-seq data obtained from leaf samples was used as EST evidence to refine the gene models. Initial gene models of protein sequences belonging to <i>Viridaeplantae</i> kingdom, obtained from the NCBI database, were used as protein evidence for refining gene prediction. Both EST and protein evidence were prepared using EXONERATE [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Slater GSC, Birney E. Automated generation of heuristics for biological sequence comparison. BMC Bioinformatics. 2005;6:31." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d28084e4919">36</a>] and used for gene prediction refinement through AUGUSTUS. All the protein sequences of these gene models were subjected to validation based on identification of homologues through BLASTP search against NRDB at E-value cutoff of 10<sup>−3</sup>. Pfam release 27 was consulted for all domain predictions with an E-value cutoff of 10<sup>−5</sup> using HMMER3 package [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Mistry J, Finn RD, Eddy SR, Bateman A, Punta M. Challenges in homology search: HMMER3 and convergent evolution of coiled-coil regions. Nucleic Acids Res. 2013;41:e121." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d28084e4927">37</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec19">Orthology detection</h3><p>All the predicted gene models from Krishna Tulsi were used with OrthoMCL tool [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Li L, Stoeckert CJ, Roos DS. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 2003;13:2178–89." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d28084e4938">38</a>] to identify clusters between selected species of <i>A. thaliana</i> (Ath), <i>O. sativa</i> (Osa), <i>S. lycopersicum</i> (Sly), <i>M. guttatus</i> (Mgu). In order to inspect distribution of the orthologous relationship of <i>Ocimum</i> genes across different species and taxonomic levels, ProteinOrtho tool [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Lechner M, Findeiss S, Steiner L, Marz M, Stadler PF, Prohaska SJ. Proteinortho: detection of (co-) orthologs in large-scale analysis. BMC Bioinformatics. 2011;12:124." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d28084e4957">39</a>] was implemented on Krishna Tulsi (Ote) gene models along with 22 different species: <i>Aquilegia caerulea</i> (Aca), <i>Glycine max</i> (Gma), <i>Setaria italic</i> (Sit), <i>Mimulus guttatus</i> (Mgu), <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i> (Sly), <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> (Ath), <i>Medicago truncatula</i> (Mtr), <i>Selaginella moellendorffii</i> (Smo), <i>Brassica rapa</i> (Bra), <i>Oryza sativa</i> (Osa), <i>Solanum tuberosum</i> (Stu), <i>Carica papaya</i> (Cpa), <i>Physcomitrella patens</i> (Ppa), <i>Theobroma cacao</i> (Tca), <i>Camellia sinensis</i> (Csi), <i>Prunus persica</i> (Ppe), <i>Vitis vinifera</i> (Vvi), <i>Eucalyptus grandis</i> (Egr), <i>Populus trichocarpa</i> (Ptr), <i>Zea mays</i> (Zma), <i>Fragaria vesca</i> (Fve), <i>Sorghum bicolor</i> (Sbi). All the complete proteome sets were obtained from Phytozome resource [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Goodstein DM, Shu S, Howson R, Neupane R, Hayes RD, Fazo J, et al. Phytozome: a comparative platform for green plant genomics. Nucleic Acids Res. 2012;40(Database issue):D1178–86." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d28084e5030">40</a>]. Phylogenetic tree reconstruction was carried out using ‘RbcS’ (Rubisco small subunit) coding sequences from all 23 species. CLUSTALW [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Larkin MA, Blackshields G, Brown NP, Chenna R, McGettigan PA, McWilliam H, et al. Clustal W and Clustal X version 2.0. Bioinformatics. 2007;23:2947–8." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d28084e5033">41</a>] and Phylip package [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Felsenstein J. PHYLIP - phylogeny inference package (version 3.2). Cladistics. 1989;5:164–6." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d28084e5036">42</a>] were employed for multiple sequence alignment (MSA) and subsequent clustering using Neighbor Joining (NJ) method, respectively. Distant homology relationships were verified through PSI-BLAST [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Altschul SF, Madden TL, Schaffer AA, Zhang J, Zhang Z, Miller W, et al. Gapped BLAST and PSI-BLAST: a new generation of protein database search programs. Nucleic Acids Res. 1997;25:3389–402." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d28084e5039">33</a>] at different set of E-value cutoffs. Gene products for which we were unable to establish any homology or orthology relationships, but consisted of a Pfam domain, were referred to as unique genes specific to Ote.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec20">Comparative analysis between Krishna Tulsi and other plant genomes</h3><p>The most recent version of whole genome sequences of <i>S. lycopersicum, V. vinefera, M. tranculata</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i> were downloaded from NCBI (<a href="ftp://ftp.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/genomes/">ftp://ftp.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/genomes/</a>). BLAT [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Kent WJ. BLAT---the BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 2002;12:656–64." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d28084e5063">43</a>] was employed for sequence searches using <i>S. lycopersicum, V. vinefera, M. tranculata</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i> genomes against two sets of Tulsi genome data: one containing 11389 scaffolds (which are greater than 10000 bp) and another containing 167 scaffolds and contigs with metabolite-related genes (identified earlier on the Krishna Tulsi genome). The figures were prepared using in-house software written for this purpose.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec21">Isolation of RNA from Tulsi subtypes, Krishna and Rama, and RNA-seq library preparation</h3><p>RNA isolation was carried out with 100 mg of the leaf tissue (Rama and Krishna) using the Sigma Spectrum Plant Total RNA Kit (STRN50, Sigma). DNA contamination was removed by DNAse treatment using DNA-free™ kit (AM1906, Ambion). The DNase free RNA quality was determined using the Agilent Bioanalyzer. The RNA Integrity Number (RIN) values of all the samples were greater than 6. The A260/280 ratio and the quantity were determined using the nanodrop. RNA-seq library preparation was done with 1 μg of total RNA following the TruSeq RNA sample preparation from Illumina (RS-122-2001).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec22">Transcriptome sequencing and assembly</h3><p>We assembled all the mRNA reads having HQ scores of all the bases more than 20, of Krishna and Rama subtype separately and also by combining the reads from both of these subtypes by using SOAPdenovo-trans [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Xie Y, Wu G, Tang J, Luo R, Patterson J, Liu S, et al. SOAPdenovo-Trans: de novo transcriptome assembly with short RNA-Seq reads. Bioinformatics. 2014;30:1660–6." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d28084e5089">26</a>] at different K-mers starting from 19 to 63 at an interval of two. An insert size of 350 was used for the assembly of transcriptomes. RNA-seq reads were mapped to the assembled genome by Tophat2 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Kim D, Pertea G, Trapnell C, Pimentel H, Kelley R, Salzberg SL. TopHat2: accurate alignment of transcriptomes in the presence of insertions, deletions and gene fusions. Genome Biol. 2013;14:R36." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d28084e5092">44</a>], which uses Bowtie2 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Langmead B, Salzberg SL. Fast gapped-read alignment with Bowtie 2. Nat Methods. 2012;9:357–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d28084e5095">45</a>] as a mapping tool. We used a minimum and maximum intron length of 50 and 500000 bp respectively. Maximum multi hits (parameter that dictates the number of alignments to the reference for a given read) was assigned as 20 and transcriptome max hits (maximum number of mappings allowed for a read, when aligned to the transcriptome) of 60 was used.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec23">Transcript differential expression comparison</h3><p>To quantify expression in terms of reads per kilo base per million (RPKM), non-redundant combined assembled transcript sequences (at 90 % sequence similarity by CD-hit EST [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Li W, Godzik A. Cd-hit: a fast program for clustering and comparing large sets of protein or nucleotide sequences. Bioinformatics. 2006;22:1658–659." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d28084e5106">46</a>]) were taken as reference. This non-redundant transcriptome was used as the reference transcriptome to calculate differential expression of transcripts in both the samples [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Góngora-castillo E, Fedewa G, Yeo Y, Chappell J, Dellapenna D, Buell CR. Genomic approaches for interrogating the biochemistry of medicinal plant species. Methods Enzymol. 2012;517:139–59." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d28084e5109">6</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Góngora-Castillo E, Buell CR. Bioinformatics challenges in de novo transcriptome assembly using short read sequences in the absence of a reference genome sequence. Nat Prod Rep. 2013;30:490–500." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d28084e5112">47</a>]. The reads of RNA-seq experiments from Krishna and Rama subtypes were mapped back on to the reference transcriptome by using SeqMap (version – 1.0.12) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Jiang H, Wong WH. SeqMap: mapping massive amount of oligonucleotides to the genome. Bioinformatics. 2008;24:2395–6." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d28084e5115">48</a>] and RPKM values were determined by using rSeq: RNA-seq analyzer (version 0.1.1) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Salzman J, Jiang H, Wong WH. Statistical modeling of RNA-seq data. Stat Sci. 2011;26:62–83." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d28084e5118">49</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec24">Specialized metabolites detection and validation</h3><p>The dataset obtained after gene prediction on the assembled genome was employed to search for enzymes involved in secondary metabolite production. There are 14 metabolites (flavonoids (2), phenylpropanoids (4), terpenes (2), sesquiterpenes (5) and sterols (1)), which are reported to be present in <i>Ocimum</i> and have known pathway information in PlantCyc (<a href="http://www.plantcyc.org/">http://www.plantcyc.org/</a>) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Plant Metabolic Network (PMN), &#xA;                    http://www.plantcyc.org/tools/tools_overview.faces&#xA;                    &#xA;                   on www.plantcyc.org, February 28, 2008. [http://www.plantcyc.org/about/citing_pmn.faces]. Date Accessed 05-08-2014." href="/articles/10.1186/s12870-015-0562-x#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d28084e5140">16</a>]. Reviewed entries from the UniProt database and all the known sequences of the enzymes from other species possessing these enzymes were used as queries to search in the full dataset of scaffolds and contigs, using PSI-BLAST at E-value of 10<sup>−5</sup> and three iterations. The protein hits obtained in our dataset were further subjected to validation using a query coverage filter of 75 %.</p><p>In order to study the expression of genes involved in the synthesis of specialized metabolite (s), the assembled transcriptome of both <i>Ocimum</i> species were searched, employing the reviewed entry corresponding to each enzyme in the UniProt database. These searches were performed using TBLASTN at an E-value of 10<sup>−3</sup>, and the best hit in our dataset was selected based on the least E-value. If the reviewed entry for any of the enzyme was not present, unreviewed entries from PlantCyc database were employed.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec25">Quantification of eugenol and ursolic acid using UHPLC-MS/SRM method</h3><p>A Vantage TSQ triple stage quadrupole mass spectrometer (Thermo Fisher Scientific, San Jose, CA, USA) equipped with a heated electro spray ionization (HESI) source was used for the analysis of eugenol and an APCI probe was used for the ursolic acid analysis. The mass spectrometer was interfaced with an Agilent 1290 infinity UHPLC system (Agilent Technologies India Pvt. Ltd., India) equipped with a column oven (set at 40 °C), auto sampler and a thermo-controller (set at 4 °C). The needle was washed from outside with acetonitrile (0.1 % formic acid) before every injection to avoid any potential carry-over problems. Separations were performed using a shim-pack XR-ODSIII column (2 × 150 mm, 2 μm). For Eugenol: Mobile phase A was water (10 mM Ammonium acetate) containing 0.1 % formic acid, and mobile phase B was acetonitrile containing 0.1 % formic acid. For Ursolic acid: Mobile phase A was water (10 mM Ammonium acetate), and mobile phase B was acetonitrile: methanol (3:1). Injections of 10 μL were performed using flow through a needle</p><ol class="u-list-style-none">
                    <li>
                      <span class="u-custom-list-number">(A)</span>
                      
                        <p>Eugenol:</p>
                      
                    </li>
                  </ol>
                        <p>Eugenol was quantified after derivatizing with pyridine sulfonyl chloride using estrone-d4 as an internal standard. Methanol was used to extract eugenol from fresh leaves (2 mg/mL) and dried stem powder (20 mg/ml). Briefly 10 μL of extract and 10 μL of internal standard (from 2.5 μg/mL) were added into 200 μL of buffer [acetone: NaHCO3 (1:1)]. To this 10 μL of pyridine sulfonyl chloride (10 mg/mL) was added and incubated at 60 °C for 15 min. After incubation the derivative was extracted with 800 μL of MTBE and the organic layer was dried and reconstituted in 50 μL of methanol followed by 10 μL injection for the analysis. A gradient (0–2 mins:30 %B, 2–5 mins:30–90 %B, 5–7 mins:90–100 %B, 7–10 mins:100 %B, 10–10.1 mins:100–30 %B, 10.1–15 mins:30) was then initiated at a flow rate of 200 μL/min. Operating conditions were as follows: spray voltage, 3000 V; ion transfer capillary temperature, 270 °C; source temperature 100 °C; sheath gas 20, auxiliary gas 5 (arbitrary units); collision gas, argon; S-lens voltage was optimized for individual metabolites; scan time of 50 millisec/transition; and ion polarity positive. A standard curve was constructed from 0.078 to 5ngon column to quantify eugenol. The SRM transition used for the analysis of eugenol is (306.1 → 79) and for estrone-d4 (416.3 → 274.1).</p><ol class="u-list-style-none">
                    <li>
                      <span class="u-custom-list-number">(B)</span>
                      
                        <p>Ursolic Acid:</p>
                      
                    </li>
                  </ol>
                        <p>Ursolic acid was quantified using estrone-d4 as an internal standard. A brief extraction was done from 2 mg/mL of dry powder using 1 mL of methanol (sonication-3 min, centrifugation −5 min). The extract was further diluted to 0.2 mg/mL in methanol. From this extract 10 μL was added along with 10 μL of internal standard (0.1 ug/mL) to 30 μL of methanol and 10 μL was injected for the analysis. A gradient (0–2 mins:20 %B, 2–8 mins:20–100 %B, 8–14.5 mins:100 %B, 14.5–14.6 mins:100–20 %B, 14.6–20 mins:20 %B) was then initiated at a flow rate of 200 μL/min. Operating conditions were as follows: Discharge current 4 μA; ion transfer capillary temperature, 270 °C; source temperature 300 °C; sheath gas 20, auxiliary gas 5 (arbitrary units); collision gas, argon; S-lens voltage was optimized for individual metabolites; scan time of 50 millisec/transition; and ion polarity positive. A standard curve was constructed from 0.034 to 2.5 ng on column to quantify ursolic acid. The same standard curve was used for the analysis of oleanolic acid. The SRM transition used for the analysis of both ursolic and oleanolic acid is (439.4 → 119) and for estrone-d4 (275.3 → 257.1).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec26">Availability of supporting data section</h3><p>Information on the genes identified in Tulsi, along with the scaffold numbers, are provided in <a href="http://caps.ncbs.res.in/Ote">http://caps.ncbs.res.in/Ote</a>.</p><p>BioProject : PRJNA251328</p><p>SRA id : SRP051184</p><p>Accession number of <i>O. tenuiflorum</i>: JQCZ00000000</p><p>Also please see DOI for supporting data: <a href="https://mynotebook.labarchives.com/share/National%2520Centre%2520for%2520Biological%2520Sciences/MTkuNXw2MjMwNC8xNS9UcmVlTm9kZS80MjAwNTk4MTM5fDQ5LjU">https://mynotebook.labarchives.com/share/National%2520Centre%2520for%2520Biological%2520Sciences/MTkuNXw2MjMwNC8xNS9UcmVlTm9kZS80MjAwNTk4MTM5fDQ5LjU</a>=</p><p>Data available from the Dryad Digital Repository: <a href="http://dx.doi.org/10.5061/dryad.6f1r2">http://dx.doi.org/10.5061/dryad.6f1r2</a>
                        </p></div></div></section>
